   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Stellar classification
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="noresize" role="img" style="width: 400px; float: right; clear: right; line-height: 1; text-align: center; background-color: #ffffff; position: relative; max-width: 100%; overflow-x: auto;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HR-diag-no-text-2.svg">
        <img alt="" data-file-height="585" data-file-width="512" height="457" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/400px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/600px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/HR-diag-no-text-2.svg/800px-HR-diag-no-text-2.svg.png 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div style="position:absolute; left:74px; top:392px">

         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div style="position:absolute; left:262px; top:184px">
        <a href="/wiki/Subgiant" title="Subgiant">

          <br/>

         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
        astronomy
       </a>
       ,
       <b>
        stellar classification
       </b>
       is the classification of
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        stars
       </a>
       based on their
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_spectrum" title="Stellar spectrum">
        spectral
       </a>
       characteristics.
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
        Electromagnetic radiation
       </a>
       from the star is analyzed by splitting it with a
       <a href="/wiki/Prism" title="Prism">
        prism
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Diffraction_grating" title="Diffraction grating">
        diffraction grating
       </a>
       into a
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">
        spectrum
       </a>
       exhibiting the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuum_(spectrum)" title="Continuum (spectrum)">
        rainbow
       </a>
       of colors interspersed with
       <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">
        spectral lines
       </a>
       . Each line indicates a particular
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">
        chemical element
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">
        molecule
       </a>
       , with the line strength indicating the abundance of that element. The strengths of the different spectral lines vary mainly due to the temperature of the
       <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">
        photosphere
       </a>
       , although in some cases there are true abundance differences. The
       <i>
        spectral class
       </i>
       of a star is a short code primarily summarizing the
       <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">
        ionization
       </a>
       state, giving an objective measure of the photosphere's temperature.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most stars are currently classified under the Morgan-Keenan (MK) system using the letters
       <i>
        O
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        F
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        G
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        K
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       , a sequence from the hottest (
       <i>
        O
       </i>
       type) to the coolest (
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       type). Each letter class is then subdivided using a numeric digit with
       <i>
        0
       </i>
       being hottest and
       <i>
        9
       </i>
       being coolest (e.g. A8, A9, F0, and F1 form a sequence from hotter to cooler). The sequence has been expanded with classes for other stars and star-like objects that do not fit in the classical system, such as class
       <i>
        D
       </i>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarfs
       </a>
       and classes
       <i>
        S
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        C
       </i>
       for
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_star" title="Carbon star">
        carbon stars
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In the MK system, a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luminosity_class" title="Luminosity class">
        luminosity class
       </a>
       is added to the spectral class using
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">
        Roman numerals
       </a>
       . This is based on the width of certain absorption lines in the star's spectrum, which vary with the density of the atmosphere and so distinguish giant stars from dwarfs. Luminosity class
       <i>
        0
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        Ia+
       </i>
       is used for
       <i>
        hypergiants
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        I
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        supergiants
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        II
       </i>
       for bright
       <i>
        giants
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        III
       </i>
       for regular
       <i>
        giants
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        IV
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        sub-giants
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       for
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-sequence" title="Main-sequence">
         main-sequence
        </a>
        stars
       </i>
       , class
       <i>
        sd
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        VI
       </i>
       ) for
       <i>
        sub-dwarfs
       </i>
       , and class
       <i>
        D
       </i>
       (or
       <i>
        VII
       </i>
       ) for
       <i>
        white dwarfs
       </i>
       . The full spectral class for the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       is then G2V, indicating a main-sequence star with a temperature around 5,800 K.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Conventional_color_description">
        Conventional color description
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Conventional color description">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Green_star_(astronomy)" title="Green star (astronomy)">
        Green star (astronomy)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TernaryColorTmap.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="441" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/TernaryColorTmap.PNG/220px-TernaryColorTmap.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/TernaryColorTmap.PNG/330px-TernaryColorTmap.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/TernaryColorTmap.PNG/440px-TernaryColorTmap.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TernaryColorTmap.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Just-saturated RGB-camera discs
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The conventional color description takes into account only the peak of the stellar spectrum. However, in actuality stars radiate in all parts of the spectrum, and because all spectral colors combined appear white, the actual apparent colors the human eye would observe are far lighter than the conventional color descriptions would suggest. This means that the simplified assignment of colors of the spectrum can be misleading. Excluding color-contrast illusions in dim light, there are no green, indigo, or violet stars.
       <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
        Red dwarfs
       </a>
       are a deep shade of orange, and
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        brown dwarfs
       </a>
       do not literally appear brown, but hypothetically would appear to be a dim grey to a nearby observer.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_classification">
        Modern classification
        <span id="MK">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Modern classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="2117" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/220px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/330px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/440px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Morgan–Keenan spectral classification
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The modern classification system is known as the
       <i>
        Morgan–Keenan
       </i>
       (MK) classification. Each star is assigned a spectral class from the older Harvard spectral classification and a luminosity class using Roman numerals as explained below, forming the star's spectral type.
      </p>
      <p>
       Other modern
       <a href="/wiki/Photometric_system" title="Photometric system">
        stellar classification systems
       </a>
       , such as the
       <a href="/wiki/UBV_photometric_system" title="UBV photometric system">
        UBV system
       </a>
       , are based on
       <a href="/wiki/Color_index" title="Color index">
        color indexes
       </a>
       —the measured differences in three or more
       <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">
        color magnitudes
       </a>
       . Those numbers are given labels such as "U-V" or "B-V", which represent the colors passed by two standard filters (e.g.
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       ltraviolet,
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       lue and
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       isual).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Harvard_spectral_classification">
        Harvard spectral classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Harvard spectral classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Harvard system
       </i>
       is a one-dimensional classification scheme by astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Annie_Jump_Cannon" title="Annie Jump Cannon">
        Annie Jump Cannon
       </a>
       , who re-ordered and simplified a prior alphabetical system. Stars are grouped according to their spectral characteristics by single letters of the alphabet, optionally with numeric subdivisions. Main-sequence stars vary in surface temperature from approximately 2,000 to 50,000
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       , whereas more-evolved stars can have temperatures above 100,000 K. Physically, the classes indicate the temperature of the star's atmosphere and are normally listed from hottest to coldest.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>

      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:H-R_diagram.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="550" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/H-R_diagram.svg/220px-H-R_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/H-R_diagram.svg/330px-H-R_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/H-R_diagram.svg/440px-H-R_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:H-R_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">
          Hertzsprung–Russell diagram
         </a>
         relates stellar classification with
         <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude" title="Absolute magnitude">
          absolute magnitude
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">
          luminosity
         </a>
         , and surface
         <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
          temperature
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The spectral classes O through M, as well as other more specialized classes discussed later, are subdivided by
       <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">
        Arabic numerals
       </a>
       (0–9), where 0 denotes the hottest stars of a given class. For example, A0 denotes the hottest stars in class A and A9 denotes the coolest ones. Fractional numbers are allowed; for example, the star
       <a href="/wiki/Mu_Normae" title="Mu Normae">
        Mu Normae
       </a>
       is classified as O9.7.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UpsOriType_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-UpsOriType-12">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       is classified as G2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunSpectrum_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SunSpectrum-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Conventional color descriptions are traditional in astronomy, and represent colors relative to the mean color of an A class star, which is considered to be white. The apparent color
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-möre_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-möre-6">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       descriptions are what the observer would see if trying to describe the stars under a dark sky without aid to the eye, or with binoculars. However, most stars in the sky, except the brightest ones, appear white or bluish white to the unaided eye because they are too dim for color vision to work. Red supergiants are cooler and redder than dwarfs of the same spectral type, and stars with particular spectral features such as carbon stars may be far redder than any black body.
      </p>
      <p>
       The fact that the Harvard classification of a star indicated its surface or
       <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">
        photospheric
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
        temperature
       </a>
       (or more precisely, its
       <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">
        effective temperature
       </a>
       ) was not fully understood until after its development, though by the time the first
       <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">
        Hertzsprung–Russell diagram
       </a>
       was formulated (by 1914), this was generally suspected to be true.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1920s, the Bengali physicist
       <a href="/wiki/Meghnad_Saha" title="Meghnad Saha">
        Meghnad Saha
       </a>
       derived a theory of ionization by extending well-known ideas in physical chemistry pertaining to the dissociation of molecules to the ionization of atoms. First he applied it to the solar chromosphere, then to stellar spectra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Harvard astronomer Cecilia Helena Payne (later to become
       <a href="/wiki/Cecilia_Payne-Gaposchkin" title="Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin">
        Cecilia Payne-Gaposchkin
       </a>
       ) then demonstrated that the
       <i>
        O-B-A-F-G-K-M
       </i>
       spectral sequence is actually a sequence in temperature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because the classification sequence predates our understanding that it is a temperature sequence, the placement of a spectrum into a given subtype, such as B3 or A7, depends upon (largely subjective) estimates of the strengths of absorption features in stellar spectra. As a result, these subtypes are not evenly divided into any sort of mathematically representable intervals.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Yerkes_spectral_classification">
        Yerkes spectral classification
        <span id="Luminosity_class">
        </span>
        <span id="Luminosity_classes">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Yerkes spectral classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dwarf_star_spectra_(luminosity_class_V)_from_Pickles_1998.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="1138" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png/220px-Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png/330px-Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png/440px-Dwarf_star_spectra_%28luminosity_class_V%29_from_Pickles_1998.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dwarf_star_spectra_(luminosity_class_V)_from_Pickles_1998.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Spectra for dwarfs (luminosity class V) for standard spectral types taken from Pickles (1998).
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
          <a href="#cite_note-17">
           [15]
          </a>
         </sup>
         Several notable spectral lines are indicated. Hydrogen lines are strongest for types A and B, and the overall spectrum peaks at shorter wavelengths for hotter stars.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Yerkes spectral classification
       </i>
       , also called the
       <i>
        MKK
       </i>
       system from the authors' initials, is a system of stellar spectral classification introduced in 1943 by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Wilson_Morgan" title="William Wilson Morgan">
        William Wilson Morgan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_Childs_Keenan" title="Philip Childs Keenan">
        Philip C. Keenan
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Edith_Kellman" title="Edith Kellman">
        Edith Kellman
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Yerkes_Observatory" title="Yerkes Observatory">
        Yerkes Observatory
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This two-dimensional (
       <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">
        temperature
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">
        luminosity
       </a>
       ) classification scheme is based on
       <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">
        spectral lines
       </a>
       sensitive to stellar temperature and
       <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">
        surface gravity
       </a>
       , which is related to luminosity (whilst the
       <i>
        Harvard classification
       </i>
       is based on just surface temperature). Later, in 1953, after some revisions of list of standard stars and classification criteria, the scheme was named the
       <i>
        Morgan–Keenan classification
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        MK
       </i>
       (by William Wilson Morgan and Philip C. Keenan's initials),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_MK_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ref_MK-19">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and this system remains the system in modern use today.
      </p>
      <p>
       Denser stars with higher surface gravity exhibit greater
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_broadening" title="Pressure broadening">
        pressure broadening
       </a>
       of spectral lines. The gravity, and hence the pressure, on the surface of a
       <a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star">
        giant star
       </a>
       is much lower than for a
       <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_star" title="Dwarf star">
        dwarf star
       </a>
       because the radius of the giant is much greater than a dwarf of similar mass. Therefore, differences in the spectrum can be interpreted as
       <i>
        luminosity effects
       </i>
       and a luminosity class can be assigned purely from examination of the spectrum.
      </p>
      <p>
       A number of different
       <b>
        luminosity classes
       </b>
       are distinguished, as listed in the table below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDS_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-CDS-20">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Marginal cases are allowed; for example, a star may be either a supergiant or a bright giant, or may be in between the subgiant and main-sequence classifications. In these cases, two special symbols are used:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A slash (
        <b>
         /
        </b>
        ) means that a star is either one class or the other.
       </li>
       <li>
        A dash (
        <b>
         -
        </b>
        ) means that the star is in between the two classes.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       For example, a star classified as A3-4III/IV would be in between spectral types A3 and A4, while being either a giant star or a subgiant.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sub-dwarf classes have also been used: VI for sub-dwarfs (stars slightly less luminous than the main sequence).
      </p>
      <p>
       Nominal luminosity class VII (and sometimes higher numerals) is now rarely used for white dwarf or "hot sub-dwarf" classes, since the temperature-letters of the main sequence and giant stars no longer apply to white dwarfs.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_peculiarities">
        Spectral peculiarities
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spectral peculiarities">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Additional nomenclature, in the form of lower-case letters, can follow the spectral type to indicate peculiar features of the spectrum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">

       </sup>
      </p>

        <td>
         Narrow ("sharp") absorption lines
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_32-6">
          <a href="#cite_note-Allen-32">
           [29]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         ss
        </th>
        <td>
         Very narrow lines
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         sh
        </th>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Shell_star" title="Shell star">
          Shell star
         </a>
         features
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_32-7">
          <a href="#cite_note-Allen-32">
           [29]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         var
        </th>
        <td>
         Variable spectral feature
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_32-8">
          <a href="#cite_note-Allen-32">
           [29]
          </a>
         </sup>
         (sometimes abbreviated to "v")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         wl
        </th>
        <td>
         Weak lines
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_32-9">
          <a href="#cite_note-Allen-32">
           [29]
          </a>
         </sup>
         (also "w" &amp; "wk")
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Element
         <br/>
         symbol
        </th>
        <td>
         Abnormally strong spectral lines of the specified element(s)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_32-10">
          <a href="#cite_note-Allen-32">
           [29]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       For example,
       <a href="/wiki/59_Cygni" title="59 Cygni">
        59 Cygni
       </a>
       is listed as spectral type B1.5Vnne,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lesh_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lesh-38">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       indicating a spectrum with the general classification B1.5V, as well as very broad absorption lines and certain emission lines.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="879" data-file-width="600" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg/170px-Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg/255px-Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg/340px-Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Star_spectra_by_Secchi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Guide for Secchi spectral types ("152 Schjellerup" is
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Y_Canum_Venaticorum" title="Y Canum Venaticorum">
          Y Canum Venaticorum
         </a>
         )
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The reason for the odd arrangement of letters in the Harvard classification is historical, having evolved from the earlier Secchi classes and been progressively modified as understanding improved.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Secchi_classes">
        Secchi classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Secchi classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       During the 1860s and 1870s, pioneering stellar spectroscopist
       <a href="/wiki/Angelo_Secchi" title="Angelo Secchi">
        Angelo Secchi
       </a>
       created the
       <i>
        Secchi classes
       </i>
       in order to classify observed spectra. By 1866, he had developed three classes of stellar spectra, shown in the table below.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late 1890s, this classification began to be superseded by the Harvard classification, which is discussed in the remainder of this article.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Class number
        </th>
        <th>
         Secchi class description
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class I
        </td>
        <td>
         White and blue stars with broad heavy
         <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_line" title="Hydrogen line">
          hydrogen lines
         </a>
         , such as
         <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">
          Vega
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Altair" title="Altair">
          Altair
         </a>
         . This includes the modern class A and early class F.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class I
         <br/>
         (Orion subtype)
        </td>
        <td>
         A subtype of Secchi class I with narrow lines in place of wide bands, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Rigel" title="Rigel">
          Rigel
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Bellatrix" title="Bellatrix">
          Bellatrix
         </a>
         . In modern terms, this corresponds to early B-type stars
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class II
        </td>
        <td>
         Yellow stars – hydrogen less strong, but evident metallic lines, such as the
         <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
          Sun
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">
          Arcturus
         </a>
         , and
         <a href="/wiki/Capella" title="Capella">
          Capella
         </a>
         . This includes the modern classes G and K as well as late class F.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class III
        </td>
        <td>
         Orange to red stars with complex band spectra, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">
          Betelgeuse
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">
          Antares
         </a>
         .
         <br/>
         This corresponds to the modern class M.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class IV
        </td>
        <td>
         In 1868, he discovered
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon_star" title="Carbon star">
          carbon stars
         </a>
         , which he put into a distinct group:
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
          <a href="#cite_note-45">
           [41]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <br/>
         Red stars with significant
         <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
          carbon
         </a>
         bands and lines, corresponding to modern classes C and S.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         Secchi class V
        </td>
        <td>
         In 1877, he added a fifth class:
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
          <a href="#cite_note-46">
           [42]
          </a>
         </sup>
         <br/>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission-line" title="Emission-line">
          Emission-line
         </a>
         stars, such as
         <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Cassiopeiae" title="Gamma Cassiopeiae">
          Gamma Cassiopeiae
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Beta_Lyrae" title="Beta Lyrae">
          Sheliak
         </a>
         , which are in modern class Be.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">
        Roman numerals
       </a>
       used for Secchi classes should not be confused with the completely unrelated Roman numerals used for Yerkes luminosity classes.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Draper_system">
        Draper system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Draper system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="wikitable" style="float: right">
       <caption>
        Classifications in the Draper Catalogue of Stellar Spectra
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drapera_47-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-drapera-47">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draperb_48-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-draperb-48">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Secchi
        </th>
        <th>
         Draper
        </th>
        <th>
         Comment
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center;">
         I
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          A
         </b>
         ,
         <b>
          B
         </b>
         , C, D
        </td>
        <td>
         Hydrogen lines dominant.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center;">
         II
        </td>
        <td>
         E,
         <b>
          F
         </b>
         ,
         <b>
          G
         </b>
         , H, I,
         <b>
          K
         </b>
         , L
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center;">
         III
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          M
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:center;">
         IV
        </td>
        <td>
         N
        </td>
        <td>
         Did not appear in the catalogue.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         <b>
          O
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">
          Wolf–Rayet
         </a>
         spectra with bright lines.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         P
        </td>
        <td>
         Planetary nebulae.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
        </td>
        <td>
         Q
        </td>
        <td>
         Other spectra.
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="3" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          Classes carried through into the MK system are in
          <b>
           bold
          </b>
          .
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In the 1880s, the astronomer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_C._Pickering" title="Edward C. Pickering">
        Edward C. Pickering
       </a>
       began to make a survey of stellar spectra at the
       <a href="/wiki/Harvard_College_Observatory" title="Harvard College Observatory">
        Harvard College Observatory
       </a>
       , using the objective-prism method. A first result of this work was the
       <i>
        Draper Catalogue of Stellar Spectra
       </i>
       , published in 1890.
       <a href="/wiki/Williamina_Fleming" title="Williamina Fleming">
        Williamina Fleming
       </a>
       classified most of the spectra in this catalogue.
      </p>
      <p>
       The catalogue used a scheme in which the previously used Secchi classes (I to IV) were subdivided into more specific classes, given letters from A to N. Also, the letters O, P, and Q were used – O for stars whose spectra consisted mainly of bright lines, P for planetary nebulae, and Q for stars not fitting into any other class.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drapera_47-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-drapera-47">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-draperb_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-draperb-48">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Harvard_system">
        Harvard system
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Harvard system">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1897, another worker at Harvard,
       <a href="/wiki/Antonia_Maury" title="Antonia Maury">
        Antonia Maury
       </a>
       , placed the Orion subtype of Secchi class I ahead of the remainder of Secchi class I, thus placing the modern type B ahead of the modern type A. She was the first to do so, although she did not use lettered spectral types, but rather a series of twenty-two types numbered from I to XXII.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1901,
       <a href="/wiki/Annie_Jump_Cannon" title="Annie Jump Cannon">
        Annie Jump Cannon
       </a>
       returned to the lettered types, but dropped all letters except O, B, A, F, G, K, and M, used in that order, as well as P for planetary nebulae and Q for some peculiar spectra. She also used types such as B5A for stars halfway between types B and A, F2G for stars one-fifth of the way from F to G, and so on.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finally, by 1912, Cannon had changed the types B, A, B5A, F2G, etc. to B0, A0, B5, F2, etc.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is essentially the modern form of the Harvard classification system.
      </p>
      <p>
       A common
       <a href="/wiki/Mnemonic" title="Mnemonic">
        mnemonic
       </a>
       for remembering the order of the spectral type letters, from hottest to coolest, is "Oh, Be A Fine Guy/Girl, Kiss Me".
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mount_Wilson_classes">
        Mount Wilson classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Mount Wilson classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       A luminosity classification known as the Mount Wilson system was used to distinguish between stars of different luminosities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nassau1946_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nassau1946-55">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FitzGerald1969_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FitzGerald1969-56">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sandage1969_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sandage1969-57">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This notation system is still sometimes seen on modern spectra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Norris2011_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Norris2011-58">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Class
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         sd
        </td>
        <td>
         Subdwarf
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         d
        </td>
        <td>
         Dwarf
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         sg
        </td>
        <td>
         Subgiant
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         g
        </td>
        <td>
         Giant
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         c
        </td>
        <td>
         Supergiant
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spectral_types">
        Spectral types
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spectral types">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The stellar classification system is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomic" title="Taxonomic">
        taxonomic
       </a>
       , based on
       <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">
        type specimens
       </a>
       , similar to classification of species in
       <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
        biology
       </a>
       : The categories are defined by one or more standard stars for each category and sub-category, with an associated description of the distinguishing features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id=".22Early.22_and_.22late.22_nomenclature">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='"Early"_and_"late"_nomenclature'>
        "Early" and "late" nomenclature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title='Edit section: "Early" and "late" nomenclature'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="391" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/60px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/80px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png 2x" width="40"/>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text plainlist">
         Look up
         <i>
          <b>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/late-type_star" title="wiktionary:late-type star">
            late-type star
           </a>
          </b>
         </i>
         or
         <i>
          <b>
           <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/early-type_star" title="wiktionary:early-type star">
            early-type star
           </a>
          </b>
         </i>
         in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Stars are often referred to as
       <i>
        early
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        late
       </i>
       types. "Early" is a synonym for
       <i>
        hotter
       </i>
       , while "late" is a synonym for
       <i>
        cooler
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Depending on the context, "early" and "late" may be absolute or relative terms. "Early" as an absolute term would therefore refer to O or B, and possibly A stars. As a relative reference it relates to stars hotter than others, such as "early K" being perhaps K0, K1, and K3.
      </p>
      <p>
       "Late" is used in the same way, with an unqualified use of the term indicating stars with spectral types such as K and M, but it can also be used for stars that are cool relative to other stars, as in using "late G" to refer to G7, G8, and G9.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the relative sense, "early" means a lower
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_numeral" title="Arabic numeral">
        Arabic numeral
       </a>
       following the class letter, and "late" means a higher number.
      </p>
      <p>
       This obscure terminology is a hold-over from an early 20th century model of stellar evolution, which supposed that stars were powered by gravitational contraction via the
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism">
        Kelvin–Helmholtz mechanism
       </a>
       , which is now known to not apply to
       <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
        main sequence
       </a>
       stars. If that were true, then stars would start their lives as very hot "early-type" stars and then gradually cool down into "late-type" stars. This mechanism provided ages of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       that were much smaller than what is observed in the
       <a href="/wiki/Geologic_record" title="Geologic record">
        geologic record
       </a>
       , and was rendered obsolete by the discovery that stars are powered by
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
        nuclear fusion
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The terms "early" and "late" were carried over, beyond the demise of the model they were based on.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_O">
        Class O
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Class O">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/O-type_star" title="O-type star">
        O-type star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/O-type_main-sequence_star" title="O-type main-sequence star">
        O-type main-sequence star
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_giant" title="Blue giant">
        Blue giant
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant" title="Blue supergiant">
        Blue supergiant
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeta_Puppis.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Zeta_Puppis.png/220px-Zeta_Puppis.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f6/Zeta_Puppis.png 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeta_Puppis.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's rendering of
         <a href="/wiki/Zeta_Puppis" title="Zeta Puppis">
          Zeta Puppis
         </a>
         , an O4 supergiant
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       O-type stars are very hot and extremely luminous, with most of their radiated output in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        ultraviolet
       </a>
       range. These are the rarest of all main-sequence stars. About 1 in 3,000,000 (0.00003%) of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood are O-type stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some of the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_massive_stars" title="List of most massive stars">
        most massive stars
       </a>
       lie within this spectral class. O-type stars frequently have complicated surroundings that make measurement of their spectra difficult.
      </p>
      <p>
       O-type spectra formerly were defined by the ratio of the strength of the
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        He
       </a>
       II λ4541 relative to that of He I λ4471, where λ is the
       <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">
        wavelength
       </a>
       , measured in
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m" title="Ångström">
        ångströms
       </a>
       . Spectral type O7 was defined to be the point at which the two intensities are equal, with the He I line weakening towards earlier types. Type O3 was, by definition, the point at which said line disappears altogether, although it can be seen very faintly with modern technology. Due to this, the modern definition uses the ratio of the
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       line N IV λ4058 to N III λλ4634-40-42.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walborn_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walborn-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       O-type stars have dominant lines of absorption and sometimes emission for
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        He
       </a>
       II lines, prominent ionized (
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        Si
       </a>
       IV,
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        O
       </a>
       III,
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        N
       </a>
       III, and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
        C
       </a>
       III) and neutral
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        helium
       </a>
       lines, strengthening from O5 to O9, and prominent hydrogen
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balmer_lines" title="Balmer lines">
        Balmer lines
       </a>
       , although not as strong as in later types. Because they are so massive, O-type stars have very hot cores and burn through their hydrogen fuel very quickly, so they are the first stars to leave the
       <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">
        main sequence
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       When the MKK classification scheme was first described in 1943, the only subtypes of class O used were O5 to O9.5.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The MKK scheme was extended to O9.7 in 1971
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-walborn_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-walborn-64">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and O4 in 1978,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and new classification schemes that add types O2, O3 and O3.5 have subsequently been introduced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        O7V –
        <a href="/wiki/S_Monocerotis" title="S Monocerotis">
         S Monocerotis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        O9V –
        <a href="/wiki/10_Lacertae" title="10 Lacertae">
         10 Lacertae
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_B">
        Class B
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Class B">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/B-type_main-sequence_star" title="B-type main-sequence star">
        B-type main-sequence star
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Blue_giant" title="Blue giant">
        Blue giant
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_supergiant" title="Blue supergiant">
        Blue supergiant
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eta_CMa.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1650" data-file-width="1800" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eta_CMa.jpg/220px-Eta_CMa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eta_CMa.jpg/330px-Eta_CMa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Eta_CMa.jpg/440px-Eta_CMa.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eta_CMa.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of
         <a href="/wiki/Eta_Canis_Majoris" title="Eta Canis Majoris">
          Aludra
         </a>
         , a B5
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">
          supergiant
         </a>
         seen in
         <a href="/wiki/Canis_Major" title="Canis Major">
          Canis Major
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       B-type stars are very luminous and blue. Their spectra have neutral helium, which are most prominent at the B2 subclass, and moderate hydrogen lines. As
       <a href="/wiki/OB_star" title="OB star">
        O- and B-type stars
       </a>
       are so energetic, they only live for a relatively short time. Thus, due to the low probability of kinematic interaction during their lifetime, they are unable to stray far from the area in which they formed, apart from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runaway_star" title="Runaway star">
        runaway stars
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The transition from class O to class B was originally defined to be the point at which the
       <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
        He
       </a>
       II λ4541 disappears. However, with modern equipment, the line is still apparent in the early B-type stars. Today for main-sequence stars, the B-class is instead defined by the intensity of the He I violet spectrum, with the maximum intensity corresponding to class B2. For supergiants, lines of
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        silicon
       </a>
       are used instead; the Si IV λ4089 and Si III λ4552 lines are indicative of early B. At mid B, the intensity of the latter relative to that of Si II λλ4128-30 is the defining characteristic, while for late B, it is the intensity of Mg II λ4481 relative to that of He I λ4471.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walborn_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walborn-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       These stars tend to be found in their originating
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OB_association" title="OB association">
        OB associations
       </a>
       , which are associated with giant
       <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">
        molecular clouds
       </a>
       . The Orion OB1 association occupies a large portion of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiral_arm" title="Spiral arm">
        spiral arm
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">
        Milky Way
       </a>
       and contains many of the brighter stars of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_constellation" title="Orion constellation">
        constellation Orion
       </a>
       . About 1 in 800 (0.125%) of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood are
       <a href="/wiki/B-type_main-sequence_star" title="B-type main-sequence star">
        B-type main-sequence stars
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Massive yet non-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">
        supergiant
       </a>
       entities known as "Be stars" are main-sequence stars that notably have, or had at some time, one or more
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balmer_lines" title="Balmer lines">
        Balmer lines
       </a>
       in emission, with the
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
        hydrogen
       </a>
       -related
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
        electromagnetic radiation
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_spectral_series" title="Hydrogen spectral series">
        series
       </a>
       projected out by the stars being of particular interest. Be stars are generally thought to feature unusually strong
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">
        stellar winds
       </a>
       , high surface temperatures, and significant attrition of
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_mass" title="Stellar mass">
        stellar mass
       </a>
       as the objects
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_rotation" title="Stellar rotation">
        rotate
       </a>
       at a curiously rapid rate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Slettebak1988_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Slettebak1988-67">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Objects known as
       <a href="/wiki/B(e)_star" title="B(e) star">
        "B(e)" or "B[e]" stars
       </a>
       possess distinctive neutral or low ionisation
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emission_lines" title="Emission lines">
        emission lines
       </a>
       that are considered to have '
       <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_mechanism" title="Forbidden mechanism">
        forbidden mechanisms
       </a>
       ', undergoing processes not normally allowed under current understandings of
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">
        quantum mechanics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        B0V –
        <a href="/wiki/Upsilon_Orionis" title="Upsilon Orionis">
         Upsilon Orionis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B0Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Alnilam" title="Alnilam">
         Alnilam
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B2Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Chi2_Orionis" title="Chi2 Orionis">
         Chi
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
         Orionis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B2Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/9_Cephei" title="9 Cephei">
         9 Cephei
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B3V –
        <a href="/wiki/Eta_Ursae_Majoris" title="Eta Ursae Majoris">
         Eta Ursae Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B3V –
        <a href="/wiki/Eta_Aurigae" title="Eta Aurigae">
         Eta Aurigae
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B3Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Omicron2_Canis_Majoris" title="Omicron2 Canis Majoris">
         Omicron
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
         Canis Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B5Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Eta_Canis_Majoris" title="Eta Canis Majoris">
         Eta Canis Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        B8Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Rigel" title="Rigel">
         Rigel
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_A">
        Class A
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Class A">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/A-type_main-sequence_star" title="A-type main-sequence star">
        A-type main-sequence star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heic0821f.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3722" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Heic0821f.jpg/220px-Heic0821f.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Heic0821f.jpg/330px-Heic0821f.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Heic0821f.jpg/440px-Heic0821f.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heic0821f.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Fomalhaut" title="Fomalhaut">
          Fomalhaut
         </a>
         , an A3 main-sequence star
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A-type stars are among the more common naked eye stars, and are white or bluish-white. They have strong hydrogen lines, at a maximum by A0, and also lines of ionized metals (
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        Fe
       </a>
       II,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
        Mg
       </a>
       II,
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        Si
       </a>
       II) at a maximum at A5. The presence of
       <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
        Ca
       </a>
       II lines is notably strengthening by this point. About 1 in 160 (0.625%) of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood are A-type stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SIMBAD_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SIMBAD-68">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        A0Van –
        <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Ursae_Majoris" title="Gamma Ursae Majoris">
         Gamma Ursae Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A0Va –
        <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">
         Vega
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A0Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/Eta_Leonis" title="Eta Leonis">
         Eta Leonis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A0Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/HD_21389" title="HD 21389">
         HD 21389
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A1V –
        <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">
         Sirius A
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A2Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Deneb" title="Deneb">
         Deneb
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        A3Va –
        <a href="/wiki/Fomalhaut" title="Fomalhaut">
         Fomalhaut
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_F">
        Class F
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Class F">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/F-type_main-sequence_star" title="F-type main-sequence star">
        F-type main-sequence star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canopus.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1311" data-file-width="2000" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Canopus.jpg/220px-Canopus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Canopus.jpg/330px-Canopus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Canopus.jpg/440px-Canopus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canopus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Canopus" title="Canopus">
          Canopus
         </a>
         , an
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_supergiant" title="White supergiant">
          F-type supergiant
         </a>
         and the second brightest star in the night sky
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       F-type stars have strengthening spectral lines
       <i>
        H
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        K
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
        Ca
       </a>
       II. Neutral metals (
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        Fe
       </a>
       I,
       <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">
        Cr
       </a>
       I) beginning to gain on ionized metal lines by late F. Their spectra are characterized by the weaker hydrogen lines and ionized metals. Their color is white. About 1 in 33 (3.03%) of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood are F-type stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        F0IIIa –
        <a href="/wiki/Zeta_Leonis" title="Zeta Leonis">
         Zeta Leonis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        F0Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Leporis" title="Alpha Leporis">
         Alpha Leporis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        F2V –
        <a href="/wiki/78_Ursae_Majoris" title="78 Ursae Majoris">
         78 Ursae Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_G">
        Class G
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Class G">

        </a>

        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>

      </div>
