      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sun_white.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2328" data-file-width="2328" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sun_white.jpg/220px-Sun_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sun_white.jpg/330px-Sun_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Sun_white.jpg/440px-Sun_white.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sun_white.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The
         <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
          Sun
         </a>
         , a G2 main-sequence star, with dark sunspots
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       G-type stars, including the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SunSpectrum_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SunSpectrum-13">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have prominent spectral lines
       <i>
        H
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        K
       </i>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">
        Ca
       </a>
       II, which are most pronounced at G2. They have even weaker hydrogen lines than F, but along with the ionized metals, they have neutral metals. There is a prominent spike in the G band of
       <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">
        CH
       </a>
       molecules. Class G main-sequence stars make up about 7.5%, nearly one in thirteen, of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       G is host to the "Yellow Evolutionary Void".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Supergiant stars often swing between O or B (blue) and K or M (red). While they do this, they do not stay for long in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yellow_supergiant" title="Yellow supergiant">
        yellow supergiant
       </a>
       G class, as this is an extremely unstable place for a supergiant to be.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        G0V –
        <a href="/wiki/Beta_Canum_Venaticorum" title="Beta Canum Venaticorum">
         Beta Canum Venaticorum
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G0IV –
        <a href="/wiki/Eta_Bo%C3%B6tis" title="Eta Boötis">
         Eta Boötis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G0Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/Beta_Aquarii" title="Beta Aquarii">
         Beta Aquarii
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G2V –
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G5V –
        <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Ceti" title="Kappa Ceti">
         Kappa Ceti
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G5IV –
        <a href="/wiki/Mu_Herculis" title="Mu Herculis">
         Mu Herculis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G5Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/9_Pegasi" title="9 Pegasi">
         9 Pegasi
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G8V –
        <a href="/wiki/61_Ursae_Majoris" title="61 Ursae Majoris">
         61 Ursae Majoris
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G8IV –
        <a href="/wiki/Beta_Aquilae" title="Beta Aquilae">
         Beta Aquilae
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G8IIIa –
        <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Geminorum" title="Kappa Geminorum">
         Kappa Geminorum
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G8IIIab –
        <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Virginis" title="Epsilon Virginis">
         Epsilon Virginis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        G8Ib –
        <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Geminorum" title="Epsilon Geminorum">
         Epsilon Geminorum
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_K">
        Class K
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Class K">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/K-type_main-sequence_star" title="K-type main-sequence star">
        K-type main-sequence star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       "K-type star" redirects here. For the Korean nuclear fusion project, see
       <a href="/wiki/KSTAR" title="KSTAR">
        KSTAR
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arcturus_(optical).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="578" data-file-width="460" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arcturus_%28optical%29.png/220px-Arcturus_%28optical%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arcturus_%28optical%29.png/330px-Arcturus_%28optical%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Arcturus_%28optical%29.png/440px-Arcturus_%28optical%29.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arcturus_(optical).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">
          Arcturus
         </a>
         , a K1.5 giant
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       K-type stars are orangish stars that are slightly cooler than the Sun. They make up about 12% of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are also giant K-type stars, which range from
       <a href="/wiki/Hypergiant" title="Hypergiant">
        hypergiants
       </a>
       like
       <a href="/wiki/RW_Cephei" title="RW Cephei">
        RW Cephei
       </a>
       , to
       <a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star">
        giants
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supergiant" title="Supergiant">
        supergiants
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">
        Arcturus
       </a>
       , whereas
       <a href="/wiki/K-type_main-sequence_star" title="K-type main-sequence star">
        orange dwarfs
       </a>
       , like
       <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">
        Alpha Centauri
       </a>
       B, are main-sequence stars.
      </p>
      <p>
       They have extremely weak hydrogen lines, if they are present at all, and mostly neutral metals (
       <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">
        Mn
       </a>
       I,
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        Fe
       </a>
       I,
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        Si
       </a>
       I). By late K, molecular bands of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_monoxide" title="Titanium monoxide">
        titanium oxide
       </a>
       become present. There is a suggestion that K-spectrum stars may potentially increase the chances of life developing on orbiting planets that are within the habitable zone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        K0V –
        <a href="/wiki/Sigma_Draconis" title="Sigma Draconis">
         Sigma Draconis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K0III –
        <a href="/wiki/Pollux_(star)" title="Pollux (star)">
         Pollux
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K0III –
        <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Cygni" title="Epsilon Cygni">
         Epsilon Cygni
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K2V –
        <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Eridani" title="Epsilon Eridani">
         Epsilon Eridani
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K2III –
        <a href="/wiki/Kappa_Ophiuchi" title="Kappa Ophiuchi">
         Kappa Ophiuchi
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K3III –
        <a href="/wiki/Rho_Bo%C3%B6tis" title="Rho Boötis">
         Rho Boötis
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K5V –
        <a href="/wiki/61_Cygni" title="61 Cygni">
         61 Cygni A
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        K5III –
        <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Draconis" title="Gamma Draconis">
         Gamma Draconis
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_M">
        Class M
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Class M">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
        Red dwarf
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">
        Red giant
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_supergiant" title="Red supergiant">
        Red supergiant
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UY_Scuti_zoomed_in,_Rutherford_Observatory,_07_September_2014.jpeg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="684" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg/220px-UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg/330px-UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg/440px-UY_Scuti_zoomed_in%2C_Rutherford_Observatory%2C_07_September_2014.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UY_Scuti_zoomed_in,_Rutherford_Observatory,_07_September_2014.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/UY_Scuti" title="UY Scuti">
          UY Scuti
         </a>
         , an M4 supergiant
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Class M stars are by far the most common. About 76% of the main-sequence stars in the solar neighborhood are class M stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-proportions_61-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-proportions-61">
         [nb 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [nb 6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeDrew2001_11-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeDrew2001-11">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, class M main-sequence stars (
       <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
        red dwarfs
       </a>
       ) have such low luminosities that none are bright enough to be seen with the unaided eye, unless under exceptional conditions. The brightest known M-class main-sequence star is M0V
       <a href="/wiki/Lacaille_8760" title="Lacaille 8760">
        Lacaille 8760
       </a>
       , with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_magnitude" title="Stellar magnitude">
        magnitude
       </a>
       6.6 (the limiting magnitude for typical naked-eye visibility under good conditions is typically quoted as 6.5), and it is extremely unlikely that any brighter examples will be found.
      </p>
      <p>
       Although most class M stars are red dwarfs, most giants and some supergiants such as
       <a href="/wiki/VY_Canis_Majoris" title="VY Canis Majoris">
        VY Canis Majoris
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">
        Antares
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">
        Betelgeuse
       </a>
       are also class M. Furthermore, the larger, hotter
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        brown dwarfs
       </a>
       are late class M, usually in the range of M6.5 to M9.5.
      </p>
      <p>
       The spectrum of a class M star contains lines from
       <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">
        oxide
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">
        molecules
       </a>
       (in the
       <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">
        visible spectrum
       </a>
       , especially
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_monoxide" title="Titanium monoxide">
        TiO
       </a>
       ) and all neutral metals, but absorption lines of hydrogen are usually absent. TiO bands can be strong in class M stars, usually dominating their visible spectrum by about M5.
       <a href="/wiki/Vanadium(II)_oxide" title="Vanadium(II) oxide">
        Vanadium(II) oxide
       </a>
       bands become present by late M.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <b>
         Spectral standards:
        </b>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrison_59-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garrison-59">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <ul>
       <li>
        M0IIIa –
        <a href="/wiki/Beta_Andromedae" title="Beta Andromedae">
         Beta Andromedae
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        M2III –
        <a href="/wiki/Chi_Pegasi" title="Chi Pegasi">
         Chi Pegasi
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        M1-M2Ia-Iab –
        <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">
         Betelgeuse
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        M2Ia –
        <a href="/wiki/Mu_Cephei" title="Mu Cephei">
         Mu Cephei
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Extended_spectral_types">
        Extended spectral types
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Extended spectral types">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       A number of new spectral types have been taken into use from newly discovered types of stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa1010_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa1010-72">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hot_blue_emission_star_classes">
        Hot blue emission star classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hot blue emission star classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galactic_refurbishment.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1956" data-file-width="4000" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Galactic_refurbishment.jpg/220px-Galactic_refurbishment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Galactic_refurbishment.jpg/330px-Galactic_refurbishment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Galactic_refurbishment.jpg/440px-Galactic_refurbishment.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galactic_refurbishment.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=UGC_5797&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="UGC 5797 (page does not exist)">
          UGC 5797
         </a>
         , an emission-line galaxy where massive bright blue stars are formed
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
          <a href="#cite_note-73">
           [67]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Spectra of some very hot and bluish stars exhibit marked emission lines from carbon or nitrogen, or sometimes oxygen.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Class_W:_Wolf.E2.80.93Rayet">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_W:_Wolf–Rayet">
        Class W: Wolf–Rayet
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Class W: Wolf–Rayet">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">
        Wolf–Rayet star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M1-67_%26_WR124.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1318" data-file-width="1289" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/M1-67_%26_WR124.png/220px-M1-67_%26_WR124.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/M1-67_%26_WR124.png/330px-M1-67_%26_WR124.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/M1-67_%26_WR124.png/440px-M1-67_%26_WR124.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M1-67_%26_WR124.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
          Hubble Space Telescope
         </a>
         image of the nebula M1-67 and the Wolf–Rayet star
         <a href="/wiki/WR_124" title="WR 124">
          WR 124
         </a>
         in the center
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Class W or WR represents the
       <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">
        Wolf–Rayet stars
       </a>
       , notable for spectra lacking hydrogen lines. Instead their spectra are dominated by broad emission lines of highly ionized helium, nitrogen, carbon and sometimes oxygen. They are thought to mostly be dying supergiants with their hydrogen layers blown away by
       <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">
        stellar winds
       </a>
       , thereby directly exposing their hot helium shells. Class W is further divided into subclasses according to the relative strength of nitrogen and carbon emission lines in their spectra (and outer layers).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WR_36-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-WR-36">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       WR spectra range is listed below:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        WN
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WR_36-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-WR-36">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        – spectrum dominated by N III-V and He I-II lines
        <ul>
         <li>
          WNE (WN2 to WN5 with some WN6) – hotter or "early"
         </li>
         <li>
          WNL (WN7 to WN9 with some WN6) – cooler or "late"
         </li>
         <li>
          Extended WN classes WN10 and WN11 sometimes used for the Ofpe/WN9 stars
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WR_36-4">
           <a href="#cite_note-WR-36">
            [33]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          h tag used (e.g. WN9h) for WR with hydrogen emission and ha (e.g. WN6ha) for both hydrogen emission and absorption
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        WN/C – WN stars plus strong C IV lines, intermediate between WN and WC stars
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WR_36-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-WR-36">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        WC
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WR_36-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-WR-36">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        – spectrum with strong C II-IV lines
        <ul>
         <li>
          WCE (WC4 to WC6) – hotter or "early"
         </li>
         <li>
          WCL (WC7 to WC9) – cooler or "late"
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        WO (WO1 to WO4) – strong O VI lines, extremely rare
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Although the central stars of most planetary nebulae (CSPNe) show O type spectra,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tinkler_76-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tinkler-76">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       around 10% are hydrogen-deficient and show WR spectra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mizsalski_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mizsalski-77">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These are low-mass stars and to distinguish them from the massive Wolf-Rayet stars, their spectra are enclosed in square brackets: e.g. [WC]. Most of these show [WC] spectra, some [WO], and very rarely [WN].
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="The_.22Slash.22_stars">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='The_"Slash"_stars'>
        The "Slash" stars
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title='Edit section: The "Slash" stars'>
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_star" title="Slash star">
        Slash star
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        slash
       </i>
       stars are O-type stars with WN-like lines in their spectra. The name "slash" comes from their printed spectral type having a slash in it (e.g. "Of/WNL"
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walborn_62-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walborn-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a secondary group found with this spectra, a cooler, "intermediate" group designated "Ofpe/WN9".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walborn_62-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Walborn-62">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These stars have also been referred to as WN10 or WN11, but that has become less popular with the realisation of the evolutionary difference from other Wolf–Rayet stars. Recent discoveries of even rarer stars have extended the range of slash stars as far as O2-3.5If
       <sup>
        *
       </sup>
       /WN5-7, which are even hotter than the original "slash" stars.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cool_red_and_brown_dwarf_classes">
        Cool red and brown dwarf classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cool red and brown dwarf classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        Brown dwarf
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
        Red dwarf
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The new spectral types L, T, and Y were created to classify infrared spectra of cool stars. This includes both
       <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">
        red dwarfs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        brown dwarfs
       </a>
       that are very faint in the
       <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">
        visible spectrum
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        Brown dwarfs
       </a>
       , whose energy comes from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational" title="Gravitational">
        gravitational
       </a>
       attraction alone, cool as they age and so progress to later spectral types. Brown dwarfs start their lives with M-type spectra and will cool through the L, T, and Y spectral classes, faster the less massive they are; the highest-mass brown dwarfs cannot have cooled to Y or even T dwarfs within the age of the universe. Because this leads to an unresolvable overlap between spectral types
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">
        effective temperature
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">
        luminosity
       </a>
       for some masses and ages of different L-T-Y types, no distinct
       <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">
        temperature
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">
        luminosity
       </a>
       values can be given.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bdevol_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-bdevol-10">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_L">
        Class L
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Class L">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="366" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/220px-L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/330px-L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8d/L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of an L-dwarf
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Class L dwarfs get their designation because they are cooler than M stars and L is the remaining letter alphabetically closest to M. Some of these objects have masses large enough to support
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_fusion" title="Hydrogen fusion">
        hydrogen fusion
       </a>
       and are therefore stars, but most are of
       <a href="/wiki/Substellar_object" title="Substellar object">
        substellar
       </a>
       mass and are therefore brown dwarfs. They are a very dark red in color and brightest in
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        infrared
       </a>
       . Their
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       is cool enough to allow
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_hydride" title="Metal hydride">
        metal hydrides
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">
        alkali metals
       </a>
       to be prominent in their spectra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_ARAA_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kirk_ARAA-80">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_ApJ_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kirk_ApJ-81">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Due to low surface gravity in giant stars,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_monoxide" title="Titanium monoxide">
        TiO
       </a>
       - and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vanadium_monoxide" title="Vanadium monoxide">
        VO
       </a>
       -bearing condensates never form. Thus, L-type stars larger than dwarfs can never form in an isolated environment. It may be possible for these L-type supergiants to form through stellar collisions, however. An example of which is
       <a href="/wiki/V838_Monocerotis" title="V838 Monocerotis">
        V838 Monocerotis
       </a>
       while in the height of its
       <a href="/wiki/Luminous_red_nova" title="Luminous red nova">
        luminous red nova
       </a>
       eruption.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_T:_methane_dwarfs">
        Class T: methane dwarfs
        <span id="Class_T">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Class T: methane dwarfs">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/220px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/330px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png/440px-T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T-dwarf-nasa-hurt.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of a T-dwarf
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Class T dwarfs are cool
       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">
        brown dwarfs
       </a>
       with surface temperatures between approximately 550 and 1,300 K (277 and 1,027 °C; 530 and 1,880 °F). Their emission peaks in the
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        infrared
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        Methane
       </a>
       is prominent in their spectra.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_ARAA_80-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-kirk_ARAA-80">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kirk_ApJ_81-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-kirk_ApJ-81">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Classes T and L could be more common than all the other classes combined if recent research is accurate. Because brown dwarfs persist for so long—a few times the age of the universe—in the absence of catastrophic collisions these smaller bodies can only increase in number.
      </p>
      <p>
       Study of the number of
       <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">
        proplyds
       </a>
       (protoplanetary disks, clumps of gas in
       <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">
        nebulae
       </a>
       from which stars and planetary systems are formed) indicates that the number of stars in the
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">
        galaxy
       </a>
       should be several
       <a href="/wiki/Order_of_magnitude" title="Order of magnitude">
        orders of magnitude
       </a>
       higher than what was previously conjectured. It is theorized that these proplyds are in a race with each other. The first one to form will become a
       <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">
        protostar
       </a>
       , which are very violent objects and will disrupt other proplyds in the vicinity, stripping them of their gas. The victim proplyds will then probably go on to become main-sequence stars or brown dwarfs of the L and T classes, which are quite invisible to us.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_Y">
        Class Y
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Class Y">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">
        Sub-brown dwarf
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Substellar_object" title="Substellar object">
        Substellar object
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="4800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/220px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/330px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg/440px-WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WISE_1828%2B2650_Brown_dwarf.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of a Y-dwarf
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Brown dwarfs of spectral class Y are cooler than those of spectral class T and have qualitatively different spectra from them. A total of 17 objects have been placed in class Y as of August 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although such dwarfs have been modelled
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and detected within forty light-years by the
       <a href="/wiki/Wide-field_Infrared_Survey_Explorer" title="Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer">
        Wide-field Infrared Survey Explorer
       </a>
       (WISE)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa1010_72-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-nasa1010-72">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stars-cooler-than-body_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-stars-cooler-than-body-85">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cool-y-dwarf_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cool-y-dwarf-86">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       there is no well-defined spectral sequence yet and no prototypes. Nevertheless, several objects have been proposed as spectral classes Y0, Y1, and Y2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ydwarfsurvey_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ydwarfsurvey-89">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The spectra of these prospective Y objects display absorption around 1.55
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometers" title="Micrometers">
        micrometers
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-four600k-90">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Delorme et al. have suggested that this feature is due to absorption from
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       , and that this should be taken as the indicative feature for the T-Y transition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_90-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-four600k-90">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tytrans_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tytrans-91">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, this ammonia-absorption feature is the main criterion that has been adopted to define this class.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ydwarfsurvey_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ydwarfsurvey-89">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, this feature is difficult to distinguish from absorption by
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-four600k_90-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-four600k-90">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and other authors have stated that the assignment of class Y0 is premature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The latest brown dwarf proposed for the Y spectral type,
       <a href="/wiki/WISE_1828%2B2650" title="WISE 1828+2650">
        WISE 1828+2650
       </a>
       , is a &gt; Y2 dwarf with an effective temperature originally estimated around 300
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       , the temperature of the human body.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stars-cooler-than-body_85-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-stars-cooler-than-body-85">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cool-y-dwarf_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cool-y-dwarf-86">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eso1110_93-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eso1110-93">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">
        Parallax
       </a>
       measurements have, however, since shown that its luminosity is inconsistent with it being colder than ~400 K. The coolest Y dwarf currently known is
       <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 0855−0714">
        WISE 0855−0714
       </a>
       with an approximate temperature of 250 K.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luhman2016_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Luhman2016-94">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The mass range for Y dwarfs is 9–25
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       masses, but young objects might reach below one Jupiter mass, which means that Y class objects straddle the 13 Jupiter mass
       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">
        deuterium
       </a>
       -fusion limit that marks the current
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        IAU
       </a>
       division between brown dwarfs and planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ydwarfsurvey_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ydwarfsurvey-89">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Late_giant_carbon-star_classes">
        Late giant carbon-star classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Late giant carbon-star classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Carbon-stars are stars whose spectra indicate production of carbon—a byproduct of
       <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">
        triple-alpha
       </a>
       helium fusion. With increased carbon abundance, and some parallel
       <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">
        s-process
       </a>
       heavy element production, the spectra of these stars become increasingly deviant from the usual late spectral classes G, K, and M. Equivalent classes for carbon-rich stars are S and C.
      </p>
      <p>
       The giants among those stars are presumed to produce this carbon themselves, but some stars in this class are double stars, whose odd atmosphere is suspected of having been transferred from a companion that is now a white dwarf, when the companion was a carbon-star.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_C:_carbon_stars">
        Class C: carbon stars
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Class C: carbon stars">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_star" title="Carbon star">
        Carbon star
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_(data_visualisation).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3600" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg/220px-Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg/330px-Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg/440px-Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_%28data_visualisation%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Curious_spiral_spotted_by_ALMA_around_red_giant_star_R_Sculptoris_(data_visualisation).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Image of the carbon star
         <a href="/wiki/R_Sculptoris" title="R Sculptoris">
          R Sculptoris
         </a>
         and its striking spiral structure
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Originally classified as R and N stars, these are also known as
       <i>
        carbon stars
       </i>
       . These are red giants, near the end of their lives, in which there is an excess of carbon in the atmosphere. The old R and N classes ran parallel to the normal classification system from roughly mid G to late M. These have more recently been remapped into a unified carbon classifier C with N0 starting at roughly C6. Another subset of cool carbon stars are the C-J type stars, which are characterized by the strong presence of molecules of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">
        <sup>
         13
        </sup>
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">
        CN
       </a>
       in addition to those of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">
        <sup>
         12
        </sup>
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">
        CN
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A few main-sequence carbon stars are known, but the overwhelming majority of known carbon stars are giants or supergiants. There are several subclasses:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        C-R – Formerly its own class (
        <i>
         R
        </i>
        ) representing the carbon star equivalent of late G to early K-type stars.
       </li>
       <li>
        C-N – Formerly its own class representing the carbon star equivalent of late K to M-type stars.
       </li>
       <li>
        C-J – A subtype of cool C stars with a high content of
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">
         <sup>
          13
         </sup>
         C
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        C-H –
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_II" title="Population II">
         Population II
        </a>
        analogues of the C-R stars.
       </li>
       <li>
        C-Hd – Hydrogen-deficient carbon stars, similar to late G supergiants with
        <a href="/wiki/Methylidyne_radical" title="Methylidyne radical">
         CH
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_carbon" title="Diatomic carbon">
         C
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>
        </a>
        bands added.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Class_S">
        Class S
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Class S">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/S-type_star" title="S-type star">
        S-type star
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Class S stars form a continuum between class M stars and carbon stars. Those most similar to class M stars have strong
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirconium_monoxide" title="Zirconium monoxide">
        ZrO
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Spectral_bands" title="Spectral bands">
        absorption bands
       </a>
       analogous to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_monoxide" title="Titanium monoxide">
        TiO
       </a>
       bands of class M stars, whereas those most similar to carbon stars have strong
       <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">
        sodium D lines
       </a>
       and weak
       <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_carbon" title="Diatomic carbon">
        C
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </a>
       bands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Class S stars have excess amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">
        zirconium
       </a>
       and other elements produced by the
       <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">
        s-process
       </a>
       , and have more similar carbon and oxygen abundances than class M or carbon stars. Like carbon stars, nearly all known class S stars are
       <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">
        asymptotic-giant-branch
       </a>
       stars.
      </p>
      <p>
       The spectral type is formed by the letter S and a number between zero and ten. This number corresponds to the temperature of the star and approximately follows the temperature scale used for class M giants. The most common types are S3 to S5. The non-standard designation S10 has only been used for the star
       <a href="/wiki/Chi_Cygni" title="Chi Cygni">
        Chi Cygni
       </a>
       when at an extreme minimum.
      </p>
      <p>
       The basic classification is usually followed by an abundance indication, following one of several schemes: S2,5; S2/5; S2 Zr4 Ti2; or S2*5. A number following a comma is a scale between 1 and 9 based on the ratio of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirconium_monoxide" title="Zirconium monoxide">
        ZrO
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titanium_monoxide" title="Titanium monoxide">
        TiO
       </a>
       . A number following a slash is a more recent but less common scheme designed to represent the ratio of carbon to oxygen on a scale of 1 to 10, where a 0 would be an MS star. Intensities of
       <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">
        zirconium
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
        titanium
       </a>
       may be indicated explicitly. Also occasionally seen is a number following an asterisk, which represents the strength of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zirconium_monoxide" title="Zirconium monoxide">
        ZrO
       </a>
       bands on a scale from 1 to 5.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classes_MS_and_SC:_intermediary_carbon-related_classes">
        Classes MS and SC: intermediary carbon-related classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Classes MS and SC: intermediary carbon-related classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In between the M and S classes, border cases are named MS stars. In a similar way, border cases between the S and C-N classes are named SC or CS. The sequence M → MS → S → SC → C-N is hypothesized to be a sequence of increased carbon abundance with age for
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_star" title="Carbon star">
        carbon stars
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">
        asymptotic giant branch
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="White_dwarf_classifications">
        White dwarf classifications
        <span id="Class_D">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: White dwarf classifications">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_dwarf_spectroscopy" title="White dwarf spectroscopy">
        White dwarf spectroscopy
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2410" data-file-width="2209" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg/220px-Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg/330px-Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg/440px-Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sirius_A_and_B_Hubble_photo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">
          Sirius
         </a>
         A and B (a
         <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
          white dwarf
         </a>
         of type DA2) resolved by
         <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
          Hubble
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The class D (for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron-degenerate_matter" title="Electron-degenerate matter">
        Degenerate
       </a>
       ) is the modern classification used for white dwarfs - low-mass stars that are no longer undergoing
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">
        nuclear fusion
       </a>
       and have shrunk to planetary size, slowly cooling down. Class D is further divided into spectral types DA, DB, DC, DO, DQ, DX, and DZ. The letters are not related to the letters used in the classification of other stars, but instead indicate the composition of the white dwarf's visible outer layer or atmosphere.
      </p>
      <p>
       The white dwarf types are as follows:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sionspectra_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sionspectra-97">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        DA – a
        <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">
         hydrogen
        </a>
        -rich atmosphere or outer layer, indicated by strong Balmer hydrogen
        <a href="/wiki/Spectral_line" title="Spectral line">
         spectral lines
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        DB – a
        <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">
         helium
        </a>
        -rich atmosphere, indicated by neutral helium,
        <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopic_notation" title="Spectroscopic notation">
         He I
        </a>
        , spectral lines.
       </li>
       <li>
        DO – a helium-rich atmosphere, indicated by ionized helium,
        <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopic_notation" title="Spectroscopic notation">
         He II
        </a>
        , spectral lines.
       </li>
       <li>
        DQ – a
        <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
         carbon
        </a>
        -rich atmosphere, indicated by atomic or molecular carbon lines.
       </li>
       <li>
        DZ – a
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_(astronomy)" title="Metal (astronomy)">
         metal
        </a>
        -rich atmosphere, indicated by metal spectral lines (a merger of the obsolete white dwarf spectral types, DG, DK and DM).
       </li>
       <li>
        DC – no strong spectral lines indicating one of the above categories.
       </li>
       <li>
        DX – spectral lines are insufficiently clear to classify into one of the above categories.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The type is followed by a number giving the white dwarf's surface temperature. This number is a rounded form of 50400/
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       <sub>
        eff
       </sub>
       , where
       <i>
        T
       </i>
       <sub>
        eff
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Effective_temperature" title="Effective temperature">
        effective surface temperature
       </a>
       , measured in
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        kelvins
       </a>
       . Originally, this number was rounded to one of the digits 1 through 9, but more recently fractional values have started to be used, as well as values below 1 and above 9.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sionspectra_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sionspectra-97">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villanovar4_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-villanovar4-99">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Two or more of the type letters may be used to indicate a white dwarf that displays more than one of the spectral features above.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sionspectra_97-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-sionspectra-97">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        Extended white dwarf spectral types:
       </b>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sionspectra_97-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-sionspectra-97">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        DAB – a hydrogen- and helium-rich white dwarf displaying neutral helium lines.
       </li>
       <li>
        DAO – a hydrogen- and helium-rich white dwarf displaying ionized helium lines.
       </li>
       <li>
        DAZ – a hydrogen-rich metallic white dwarf.
       </li>
       <li>
        DBZ – a helium-rich metallic white dwarf.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       A different set of spectral peculiarity symbols are used for white dwarfs than for other types of stars:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Code
        </th>
        <th>
         Spectral peculiarities for stars
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         P
        </th>
        <td>
         Magnetic white dwarf with detectable polarization
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         E
        </th>
        <td>
         Emission lines present
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         H
        </th>
        <td>
         Magnetic white dwarf without detectable polarization
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         V
        </th>
        <td>
         Variable
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         PEC
        </th>
        <td>
         Spectral peculiarities exist
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Stellar_remnants">
        Stellar remnants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Stellar remnants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        Neutron star
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        Black hole
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Exotic_star" title="Exotic star">
        Exotic star
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Stellar remnants are objects associated with the death of stars. Included in the category are white dwarfs, and as can be seen from the radically different classification scheme for class D, non-stellar objects are difficult to fit into the MK system.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Hertzsprung-Russell diagram, which the MK system is based on, is observational in nature so these remnants cannot easily be plotted on the diagram, or cannot be placed at all. Old
       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">
        neutron stars
       </a>
       are relatively small and cold, and would fall on the far right side of the diagram.
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">
        Planetary nebulae
       </a>
       are dynamic and tend to quickly fade in brightness as the progenitor star transitions to the
       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">
        white dwarf
       </a>
       branch. If shown, a planetary nebula would be plotted to the right of the diagram's upper right quadrant. A
       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">
        black hole
       </a>
       emits no visible light of its own, and therefore would not appear on the diagram.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan-var-hr_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-chan-var-hr-100">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Replaced_spectral_classes">
        Replaced spectral classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Replaced spectral classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Several spectral types, all previously used for non-standard stars in the mid-20th century, have been replaced during revisions of the stellar classification system. They may still be found in old editions of star catalogs: R and N have been subsumed into the new C class as C-R and C-N.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">

        <li style="display:table-row">
         <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em;vertical-align:middle;text-align:center">

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Stellar_classification&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-5">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-5">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           This is the relative color of the star if
           <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">
            Vega
           </a>
           , generally considered a bluish star, is used as a standard for "white".
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-9">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-9">

            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">

           <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
            Sun
           </a>
           (a G2 star) is white, while a G9 star is yellow.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-29">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

            </a>
           </b>
          </span>

          </span>
         </li>

           <a href="/wiki/Ap_and_Bp_stars" title="Ap and Bp stars">

           </a>

          </span>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="References">
        References
       </span>
